B0058YPL66|5.0|Once Upon a Time is a heady cocktail of fairy tales, children's lit and Disney animated movies.<br /><br />As it begins, the Evil Queen of Snow White fame makes her bid for revenge by unleashing a spell on all the residents of Once Upon a Time land - including her nemeses Snow White and Prince Charming - which will hurl them into our world sans memories, ergo stripping them of their happiness.  A council of the good guys manage to gum up the works by sending Snow and Charming's literally just born baby daughter Emma through first, making her the only person who can break the curse.<br /><br />Flash forward to the modern town of Storybrooke, Maine.  Emma is now a young woman who got herself knocked up and, unable to care for her son presumably because of a combination of her young age at the time and the unwillingness of her lover to step up to the plate, had given him up for adoption.  This is something she was at peace with until the boy, Henry, seeks her out with a wild story: he is the adoptive son of Storybrooke's mayor Regina - none other than the Evil Queen herself - and the whole town consists of cursed characters who cannot remember their identities and cannot cross the town border and whom Emma is obligated to save.  Occasionally aided by town residents Mary Margaret (Snow), David (Charming), Archie (Jiminy Cricket), Ruby (Little Red Riding Hood) and Ruby's Grandma, Emma begins to oppose Regina, not quite believing Henry but also not liking what she sees in Regina's behavior and becoming attached to Henry in spite of her intentions.  But the Evil Queen has no intention of letting the curse be lifted, and also in the mix is the perhaps even worse Rumpelstiltskin, who starts racking up favors for purposes of his own...<br /><br />Can Emma save the residents of Storybrooke, including her unknown parents and the son she thought she wasn't cut out for raising?<br /><br />Some of the characters are immediately recognizable, but half of the fun is figuring out whoalot of the town residents are going to turn out to be - particularly town Sheriff Graham, who deputizes Emma and backs her up, and later on a biker who eventually goes by the name of August and seems to know what's going on.  Many of the backstories turn the traditional views of the characters completely on their heads.  Sometimes it's with clever whimsey, but other times with pitch blackness and in some cases aching tragedy.  The Queen's damage runs deep and far, and there are several stories of couples and relatives torn apart and trying to find their way back to eachother for a happily ever after - even if they don't quite know who they are thanks to the curse.  There are any number of people to root for and get attached to, with some having been played a worse hand than others and many trying to rectify mistakes they've made.  Acting honors almost definitely go to Robert Carlyle, who does a phenomenal job as a Rumpelstiltskin who manages to be sympathetic, creepy, scary and his own worst enemy all at once; but there are nuggets for basically all the main and supporting cast.<br /><br />If you're a fractured fairy tale fan, then this is a must watch.

B006Z476J8|5.0|*** THIS REVIEW IS FOR THE 2012 3CD 'DELUXE EDITION' ***<br /><br />Monday 7 May 2012 sees the UK release of a whopping four DELUXE EDITIONS for Small Faces fans (15 May 2012 in the USA) - and for many their 1968 masterpiece \\"Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake\\" (a Number 1 album in the UK) will be 'the' jewel in a very tasty set of crowns.<br /><br />Here are the finite details for Universal/Sanctuary 276 523-5:<br /><br />Disc 1 (38:30 minutes):<br />Tracks 1 to 12 are the MONO version of \\"Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake\\" - their fourth studio album released 24 May 1968 in the UK on Immediate Records IMLP 012<br /><br />Disc 2 (41:17 minutes):<br />Tracks 1 to 14 are all PREVIOUSLY UNRELEASED (Track 8 Previously Unreleased on CD)<br />1. Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake (Early Session Version - Mono)<br />2. Afterglow (Alternate USA Mix - Stereo)<br />3. Long Agos And Worlds Apart (Alternate USA Mix - Stereo)<br />4. Rene, The Dockers Delight (Early Session Mix - Stereo)<br />5. Song Of A Baker (Alternate USA Mix - Stereo)<br />6. Lazy Sunday (Alternate USA Mix - Stereo)<br />7. Happiness Stan (Backing Track - Mono)<br />8. Bun In The Oven (Early Session Mix - Mono)<br />9. The Fly (Take 4 - Instrumental Version - Stereo)<br />10. Mad John (Take 7 - Early Session Version - Stereo)<br />11. HappyDaysToyTown (Alternate USA Mix - Stereo)<br />12. Kamikhazi (Take 7 - Backing Track - Mono)<br />13. Every Little Bit Hurts (Early Session Mix - Mono)<br />14. Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake (Alternate Take - Phased Mix - Stereo)<br /><br />Disc 3 (38:21 minutes):<br />Tracks 1 to 12 are the STEREO version of \\"Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake\\" - on Immediate Records IMSP 012<br />[Note: in advance of the album \\"Lazy Sunday\\" b/w \\"Rollin' Over\\" was released 5 April 1968 as a 7\\" single in the UK on Immediate IM O64 (mono mixes above).<br />\\"Afterglow\\" was also released as 7\\" single in the UK (credited as \\"Afterglow Of Your Love\\") in March 1969 on Immediate IM 077 with the non-album track \\"Wham Bam Thank You Man\\" as its B-side.<br />That song is NOT on here]<br /><br />PACKAGING:<br />Since Universal stopped using titled plastic slip-around wraps on their DELUXE EDITIONS - they've plumed instead for a bit of sticky tape at the base that you have to cut and split to get open - not the most graceful of presentations it has to be said. But once inside the detail is impressive. As fans will know \\"Ogdens'...\\" was released in the most beautiful and elaborate artwork designed by Mick Swan - a foldout five-flaps circular-sleeve based on a Victorian tobacco tin (name changed by the boys to avoid copyright infringement). Folded out in full - it featured two colour prints on the inside and 4 black and white snaps of the band on the other flaps. The Mono and Stereo CDs contain 'variants' of the gorgeous colour prints by Pete Brown and Nick Tweddell (the actual LP ones are in the booklet) while Gered Mankowitz's black and white 'spider's web' snaps of the band that filled each of the other circles are reproduced on the card flaps. Beneath the two see-through CD trays are repros of the labels for Side 1 and Side 2 of the original 1968 Stereo LP.<br /><br />The booklet is lovely - 24 colour pages filled with Immediate memorabilia, rare worldwide 7\\" picture sleeves for both \\"Lazy Sunday\\" and \\"Afterglow Of Your Love\\" and even a battered-looking Scotch tape box. The liner notes by MARK PAYTRESS are typically informative and fact-filled (such a good writer) and give you great insights into the album's formation, Stanley Unwin's \\"Unwinese\\" gobbledegook English that fills Side 2's \\"Happiness Stan\\" suite and indeed the general creative mayhem that surrounded this most beloved of English albums. I particularly like the Immediate Records trade advert that uses a re-wording of \\"Our Father\\" (The Lord's Prayer) to sell the LP (\\"...and deliver us Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake...\\" etc) - blinding!<br /><br />SOUND:<br />But the real meat for fans will be the NEW REMASTERS from best-ever sources and overseen by surviving members of the band - KENNEY JONES and IAN McLAGAN. Tape Research and Recovery is by ROB CAIGER, Tape Restoration and Transfer from Analogue is by ROB KAYLACH and Mastering by NICK ROBBINS - and man have they collectively done a bang-up job. Little will prepare fans for the sonic whack off this...<br /><br />\\"Ogdens'...\\" for me has always been a two-sided production job - Side 1 is good - but Side 2 is stupendous - and that feeling continues on this reissue. The extensive use of guitar 'phasing' on Side 1 gives the tracks that evocative 60t's feel for sure - but it also sounds sonically compromised somehow - Side 2 does not. Take the strings and opening Stanley Unwin dialogue on \\"Rollin' Over\\" (his words title this review) - it's unbelievably clear and full of presence. Then when the band does kick in with that riff and harmonica blasting - it's awesome. The drums and acoustic guitars that open \\"Mad John\\" are HUGE - as is the drums and organ on the STEREO mix of \\"The Journey\\". The last remaster I had was good - but this is so much better and musically sweeter. And \\"Lazy Sunday\\" has never sounded so glorious. \\"Song Of A Baker\\", \\"Rene\\" - it's all a triumph...<br /><br />UNRELEASED:<br />A \\"...1, 2, 3, 4...\\" vocal count-in gives us a stringed-up and heavily phased \\"Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake\\" that is fascinating - and even in its unfinished form - sounds extraordinary - like something from another world. Unfortunately you can understand why the 'Alternate USA Stereo' mixes have stayed in the can - Marriott's vocals echoed to a ridiculous degree in the mix - almost to a point where it's all you hear. Better is the 'Early Session Mix' of \\"Rene\\" which accentuates the drums and sees the piano and organ stretch out (the lyrics still make me laugh). The opening plucked strings of \\"Happiness Stan\\" sound bare without Unwin's witty vocals following - but I love the harpsichord and keyboard playing to the fore as the song progresses. \\"Bun In The Oven\\" turns out to be an early session version of \\"Rollin' Over\\" which is rougher than the finished article but just as powerful. Even in rehearsal mode Steve Marriott lays into Brenda Holloway's Motown classic \\"Every Little Bit Hurts\\" with great results and feeling. \\"The Fly\\" is an acoustic instrumental version of \\"The Hungry Intruder\\" with a count-in and along with another band-instrumental \\"Kamikhazi\\" (so-funky-Small Faces) for me are the highlights on here.<br /><br />NIGGLES:<br />The cracking non-album B-side to \\"Afterglow Of Your Love\\" is \\"Wham Bam Thank You Man\\" - but it's nowhere to be seen. Nor is the near 7-minute Alternate Stereo version of \\"Wham...\\" that turned up on the \\"Darlings Of Wapping Wharf Launderette\\" 2CD set in 1999 - strange omissions to say the least. Maybe they're been kept back for that other missing piece in the jigsaw - the Immediate Records double-album \\"Autumn Stone\\" from 1969? Most fans will already have \\"Wham Bam...\\" track from preceding comps of course - but it's a point worth making. And bluntly I miss the sheer visual impact of the vinyl album artwork...(vinyl is due in full repro glory soon).<br /><br />To sum up - while Disc 2 may not exactly set the Universe on fire - there are some unheard nuggets worth the admission price. But for me it's the brilliantly realized remasters that thrill the most. The MONO mix has extraordinary power (apparently the band's preferred version) and tracks like \\"Afterglow\\" on the STEREO disc now sound amazing (with previous errors fixed). It all equates to real effort applied - finally getting this right. Both Jones and McLagan are to be praised for keeping the recorded legacy of Steve Marriott and Ronnie Lane alive - and in such style.<br /><br />What a band - and what an album. 44 years on and it still blows you away.<br /><br />\\"What up man!\\" indeed.mbers of the band - KENNEY JONES and IAN McLAGAN. Tape Research and Recovery is by ROB CAIGER, Tape Restoration and Transfer from Analogue is by ROB KAYLACH and Mastering by NICK ROBBINS - and man have they collectively done a bang-up job. Little will prepare fans for the sonic whack off this...<br /><br />\\"Ogdens'...\\" for me has always been a two-sided production job - Side 1 is good - but Side 2 is stupendous - and that feeling continues on this reissue. The extensive use of guitar 'phasing' on Side 1 gives the tracks that evocative 60t's feel for sure - but it also sounds sonically compromised somehow - Side 2 does not. Take the strings and opening Stanley Unwin dialogue on \\"Rollin' Over\\" (his words title this review) - it's unbelievably clear and full of presence. Then when the band does kick in with that riff and harmonica blasting - it's awesome. The drums and acoustic guitars that open \\"Mad John\\" are HUGE - as is the drums and organ on the STEREO mix of \\"The Journey\\". The last remaster I had was good - but this is so much better and musically sweeter. And \\"Lazy Sunday\\" has never sounded so glorious. \\"Song Of A Baker\\", \\"Rene\\" - it's all a triumph...<br /><br />UNRELEASED:<br />A \\"...1, 2, 3, 4...\\" vocal count-in gives us a stringed-up and heavily phased \\"Ogdens' Nut Gone Flake\\" that is fascinating - and even in its unfinished form - sounds extraordinary - like something from another world. Unfortunately you can understand why the 'Alternate USA Stereo' mixes have stayed in the can - Marriott's vocals echoed to a ridiculous degree in the mix - almost to a point where it's all you hear. Better is the 'Early Session Mix' of \\"Rene\\" which accentuates the drums and sees the piano and organ stretch out (the lyrics still make me laugh). The opening plucked strings of \\"Happiness Stan\\" sound bare without Unwin's witty vocals following - but I love the harpsichord and keyboard playing to the fore as the song progresses. \\"Bun In The Oven\\" turns out to be an early sessionversion of \\"Rollin' Over\\" which is rougher than the finished article but just as powerful. B000YI7MRM|5.0|I posted this review today on amazon.uk. It seems that the film has also suffered from the same type of critics on Amazon.com. Accordingly, I felt it might be useful - at least for some - to read a somewhat different point of view coming from someone who really loved the film.<br /><br />I am a bit amazed that this film has been critized so much, and this is why I am reviewed it only now, after having bought it over six months ago, seen it twice, and thoroughly enjoyed enjoyed it each time.<br /><br />As another recent has noted (Vandal), I also like it both despite of and because of the main criticism levelled against it: that it is disjointed, with, allegedly, too much crammed into one film. Stating that the film includes bits and pieces from three of Perez-Reverte's books on Captain Alatriste is quite correct. However, this did not \\"ruin any dramatic potential\\" for me. Quite the opposite in fact, it added some. Rather than a continuous, linear and somewhat conventional story of Captain Alatriste over some 20 years (from 1622 to the battle of Rocroi in 1643), you get glimpses, the main episodes in the life of a profesional and veteran Tercio. Whether this is deliberatly intended or whether it is just the result of wanting to include as much of the three books as possible is rather irrelevant to me. This is because I found the end result rather good and, unlike others perhaps, I do not necessarily need my films to spell everything out.<br /><br />Interestingly, I also felt that, by concentrating on the issue above, most reviewers seem to have missed what for me was the main point of the whole film: the hero's (or should I have written the \\"anti-hero\\"?) rise and fall, which mirrors that of Spain. Two other reviewers have captured parts - but only parts - of this core theme that makes the film come together.<br /><br />One is Charles Vasey in ghis \\"Golden Century\\" review. This is indeed a film about the Spanish wars in Flanders against the heretics and rebels that would become the Dutch: heretics because they were Protestants, and rebels because they rejected the King of Spain's authority and were fighting for independence which they would finally obtain, becoming the United Provinces (the current Netherlands). These endless wars lasted the best part of 80 years and were quite horrific, even for the time, as the film shows rather well with its episode on canal warfare and the other episode on the siege of Breda. Spain, who would finally be defeated a good 20 years after Rocroi (in 1643), would also be bankcrupted and bled dry by the endless war, while the Dutch received support from England, and even sometimes from Catholic France, both bitter rivals of Spain. This is largely seen in the film.<br /><br />The other reviewer (Bob Salter) rather insisted on Alatriste as a \\"killer for a few coins\\". This is the other part of the story, that of an increasingly impoverished - or at least unequal Spain where demobilized soldiers were no longer paid and had to hire out their swords to anyone willing to pay them the few coins that would precent them from starving, while the great nobles intrigued, plotted and got ever richer. The film's contrast between the arrogant and despising attitude of Olivares (very well portrayed, by the way) and the impoverished but still proud Alatriste is one of the most powerful scenes. Reduced to misery, Alatriste, and all of the other demobilized soldiers who only have their swords to live on, are simply ready to accept ANY job to survive, especially killing somebody.<br /><br />One the object lessons of the film is to show how the most valiant and brave of Spain's sons, those that bled and died on its numerous battlefields, were the most poorly and dishonorably treated, as if they were beggars, when they went home and were no longer fighting a war. So, it is hardly surprising if some reviewers found the film gloomy: it's all about misery, doom and gloom, on the one hand, and herosm and honor (but NOT glory) on the other. The first piece occurrs whenever Alatriste is demobilized (and therefore not paid anything anymore by the Crown). The second, when he is fighting for the Greater glory and benefit of his (somewhat unworthy) King. These contrasts are, of course, quite deliberate in the film, and also exist in the books. This is, after all, the Spanish and modern version of the Three Musketeers: it's realistic and feels very real. It's about doom, gloom and honour, and don't expect any \\"Hollywodian happy ending\\". There are a couple of love stories, of course, but they do not end particularly well and the couples do not exactly end up by ridding of into the sunset!<br /><br />Finally, there is, of course, the costums, which are wonderfully accurate to the extent that they seem to be straight out of a museum (the Prado, of course!), the actors' performances, on which so much has already been said that I do not feel the need to add more comments (I loved Vigo Mortensen's performance, but I also liked many of the others, starting with the actor playing Olivares) and the battles and duels, which are filmed in such a way as to make them as gripping and as realistic as possible. The last scenes - those of the battle of Rocroi - are particularly good, and perfectly historical by the way: the Tercios did not surrender and fought to the last before being overrun by the French cavalry. The film's end, which does stop abruptly, is certainly deliberate: either there was nothing more to show, apart from the final slaughter, or this was allowing for a Part 2 sometime in the future. After all, the film only uses Perez-Reverte's first three books on Alatriste, so there is still room for at least one sequel...<br /><br />Anyway, in case you had not realized yet, I loved that film and it worked fine for me...y) on the other. B003VPWX6K|3.0|\\"The Remains of the Day\\", a novel written by Kazuo Ishiguro was awarded the 1989 Booker Prize in literature.  Ishiguro was born in Nagasaki, Japan in 1954 and migrated to Britain in 1960.  He was last reported living in London.  Ishiguro has two other notable works;  \\"A Pale View of the Hills\\", his first novel, won the Winifred Holtby Prize of the Royal Society of Literature; his second novel, \\"An Artist of the Floating World\\" won the 1958 Whitbread Book of the Year Award.<br /><br />Ishiguro's novel \\"The Remains of the Day\\" is narrated in the first person by the main character of the work.  The writing is not complex and provides easy reading.  The setting of the novel takes place in England around the beginning of the rise of Germany to power in the 1940's.  It is a story of a \\"manservant\\", a butler by the name of Stevens, who plies his trade for nearly four decades in the household of one Darlington Hall, a grand mansion serving Lord Darlington a well known and influential personage during the period. After the death of Lord Darlington, the household that had been in the Darlington family for almost two centuries, transfers to a new owner, an American, a Mr. Farraday who becomes the new master and Mr. Stevens' current employer.  The story picks up in the present, with Mr. Farraday suggesting a road trip for Mr. Stevens to which the butler accepts; and thus begins, during the trip, a recounting of Stevens' time and events that happened in Darlington Hall.<br /><br />This was a most peculiar composition to read.  The life of a butler is hardly an engaging topic to have chosen to write about.  As I was reading, I distinctly remember deciding that the novel was not very stimulating, even boring.  However, to my surprise I was compelled to keep reading; the exact reason now eludes me.  I kept thinking that if the story had been written from the point of view of a household companion, a canine perhaps, that it would have sounded much the same. One could easily substitute a dog's \\"loyalty\\" for Stevens' \\"dignity\\". The butler, Mr. Stevens' was a man to be pitied because of his inability to elevate himself to a human level.  Perhaps this was the catalyst that kept me reading - I was always expecting to finally see Stevens' elevation from the mundane to the sublime; in the end that did not happen although there were glimpses of recognition along the way.<br /><br />All and all I was not particularly taken in by this composition. It dragged on with no climatic episode. The story of Mr. Stevens ended pretty much the way it began - a melancholy view of a man whose repertoire was limited to serving and whose humanity never elevated itself to importance.<br />I recommend this novel with reservations as I don't think it will suit everyone in its subject or anticlimactic pursuits. There is a degree of existentialism underlying the narrative, however it does not seem contemporary and may not be enough to engage the reader.

I often wished I had a timeline to review as I read, but then I learned to let go and allow the story to reveal itself on its own terms.<br /><br />This one is for adults, as much for its graphic depictions of sex and violence as for its subtlety in watching the players manipulate each other. I recommend A Game of Thrones.

1451673442|4.0|Since this is a book about the magic of gratitude, it's hard to not give it a 5-star review (as I see most have done here!) because it seems ungrateful. But I want to be honest about what I perceive to be the limitations of this book. On the plus side, it is a straightforward and good-feeling book about the power of gratitude, with many exercises for how to bring more gratitude into your awareness regarding every area of your life, including health, relationships, family, money, career, and spirituality. It is presented within the context of the law of attraction, since it is part of the series of books begun with the author's [[ASIN:1582701709 The Secret]]. The premise is that through gratitude, we change what we attract - we attract more of what we are grateful for, rather than attracting from a place of 'lack'.<br /><br />I support this view, and have seen many examples in my own life. Although for me there was nothing new in this book, it did shift me into a more grateful place, and did lift my attention, which counts for a lot to me. But I found it overly simplistic and repetitive. It didn't get into more complicated situations, which often arise in real life - when we are grateful for something that is actually bad for us, or truly have mixed emotions regarding something, or can feel gratitude but still at some level feel undeserving - all of which also impact what we attract. Like The Secret, it also assumes the law of attraction is the only law operating, which also leads to oversimplification. Other authors have gone into more depth about these themes, and I was hoping for a little more here, since it's the third in this series. But if you liked the first 2, and so know what you are getting, you will probably appreciate this one too.

1401235417|5.0|In every comic characters catalog, they have at least one event that either changes the characters image in a defining factor or is just so well done the whole world is taking notice of it while it's happening.  For someone like Batman, he's had his share of both of those qualities numerous times in his lifetime.  Batman: The Dark Knight Returns, Batman: Year One, Batman: A Death in the Family, and Batman: The Long Halloween or Batman: Knightfall saga, are just a few examples of such stories that come to mind.  We might be adding another story to that list in the future.  Ever since writer Scott Snyder has come on into the comic scene as a relative little known until his run on The Black Mirror, the man has become synonymous with Batman.  BUT...his run was with Dick Grayson as Batman.  Not Bruce Wayne.  With the DC New 52, Snyder has become a hot community now and has been given the chance to tackle Bruce Wayne as Batman for the first time ever.  And let's just say, it's one hell of an introduction.  I'll try to keep it spoiler free.<br /><br />BATMAN VOL.1: THE COURT OF OWLS collects issues #1-7 and finds Bruce Wayne back in Gotham, as well as returning to the title of Batman *(everything before the New 52 reboot carry over in Batman.  But don't worry, you don't need to have read any of that to understand or enjoy it this book.  So new readers should have no problem coming in to this story, even with this little plot detail.)*  Batman and Bruce Wayne are on an all-time high: Batman is completely self assured of his position since returning (he just about effortlessly defeats most of his rouges gallery within a jailbreak at Arkham asylum)  and Bruce Wayne is looking into the future.  Gone is Wayne being haunted by the past and loneliness, now replaced with a positive attitude and outlook into rebuilding a bright shining future for Gotham.  Added to his cause, a new candidate for mayor that believes in Bruce's cause as well.  But things start happening weirdly in Gotham when the appearance of a deadly assassin by the name The Talon shows up and demonstrates incredible skills that almost rival Batman.  The Talon also mentions the court of owls, an urban legend/bedtime story that starts creeping its head into the larger influence on Gotham that Batman ever though possible.<br /><br />The Court of Owls Vol.1 is something of a precursor to a summer blockbuster and the famous Knightfall saga.  The main villain is new and shows it's every bit as up to the level of Batman himself, the side effects of working alone, and yes folks...Batman almost physically and mentally gets pushed to the limit (the sold-out issue #5 is something you will truly be stunned by in this regard).  All the while bits and pieces Snyder has built up on since Gates of Gotham (and even The Black Mirror) about Gotham's legacy finally come to ahead with the threat of The Talon, combined with the Court of Owls themselves, is something of such dread and scope we haven't feel for since Knightfall and No Man's Land.  This is truly epic in perception and depth in Batman that might find its place in the Bat mythos years to come (if Vol.2 ends up just as well as Vol.1, for example).<br /><br />If your one who's become a fan of Snyder's work in Batman, such as Scott Snyder's other Bat-book Pre-52 was the Black Mirror, which was a dark, horror-like take with Dick Grayson as Batman...Snyder's take on Bruce Wayne is more suspenseful, action-packed sort of history lesson on Gotham.  This makes Court of Owls a very different beast then Black Mirror.  So if your one who loved BM, you might quite not be into CoO's as much (though I doubt it.  Snyder's work is still solid).  And if you're a new reader, this will be a great jumping on point (the other Snyder Bat-book is Gates of Gotham which isn't necessary, but still an interesting read).<br /><br />Greg Cappullo's art work flourishes here that is a mix of sketchy and precision at the same time.  I've seen his work from years of doing Spawn, but it never awed me in anyway.  I like his art; I just never was blown away from him.  His current work for Batman has changed my mind.  His artistry holds up just fine without seeming scary or too simple.  His highest compliment for his art narrative, combined with Snyder's script comes to ahead in issue #5 for example (again, issue #5 is an exceptional issue).<br /><br />If there's any faults I can think of, there minor and personal on my behalf.  I feel Batman is a tinny-bit too arrogant then I think he would be or I can remember in most Batman books.  Also his attitude toward much of the Bat-family feels disconnected for no reason at all, considering Batman's attitude toward his fellow Bat-crime fighters has been reasonably tight ever since Jason Todd's death (his rebirth doesn't change this notion).  The other thing is Batman is considered the worlds greatest detective, yet --*SPOILER*-- He disregards the Court of Owls existence.  Even worse, after being attacked by The Talon and finding evidence of The Court itself...he still denies them.--*SPOLIER END*--.  It just doesn't sit right with me on that information, but Snyder backs up these problems I have as well.  Batman's arrogance stems from single-handedly taking out his entire Rouges gallery, so this does seem possible.*SPOILER*  And my qualms with Bat's denial of The Court are told by Snyder by through a flashback explaining his reasons for denying the Court*SPOILER END*.  So my problems are counteracted by Snyder's explanations (and even furthered when Vol.2 comes out), added with so many of the positives that I still keep my score at a perfect 5.<br /><br />BATMAN VOL.1: THE COURT OF OWLS is an event that is fresh, exciting, epic, mysterious, and just plain fun.  Heck, I bought the single issues upon release and bought this collected book because I enjoyed it so much.  Old Batman readers will easily enjoy this (considering most fans accepted Snyder from his work on The Black Mirror) and new readers will have a blast coming into a new legacy of the Batman-lore.  Hopefully, just hopefully the entire arc that finishes off in Vol.2 known as the \\"Night of the Owls\\" will be as powerful as Vol.1.  Either way, this is a great book that I highly recommend.oing Spawn, but it never awed me in anyway.  I like his art; I just never was blown away from him. 0439023513|5.0|It was really hard for me to determine how many stars to give this book. I was torn in the 3-5 range, and I had to spend some time thinking, and reading other reviews of it first. It was hard to separate the very dark, drained, depressed feeling it gave me from the real quality of the story. The fact that it made me feel like crap did not mean it was a bad book. I reminded myself that this was a dystopia. But then, I thought, I LOVED The Handmaid's Tale and 1984. They didn't make me feel like crap, after I read them. I came away thinking they were just amazing books. So, what was the difference between those books and this one? I wasn't emotionally attached to any of the characters in those two books.<br /><br />I spent the first portion of the book really stressed out, worrying about Peeta. Unlike the way I've seen a lot of fans describe this series, I never saw it as a true love triangle between Peeta and Gale. I thought by the end of Catching Fire it was clear that it was all Peeta. It was shown, not told. I never got attached to Gale. In Mockingjay, I finally had the chance to get to know him. And the more I learned of him, the more I disliked him. And I picked up on Katniss having some of the same discomfort I had with him.<br /><br />**MILD SPOILERS**<br /><br />And once Peeta became part of the story again, I was even more stressed out than I was before. What happened there, I've seen it done in other stories, and it always gets to me.  There are other instances in fiction of the love interest, or close friend, turning against the main character. No, the fact that it's been done before is not a criticism, because as they say, \\"There's nothing new under the sun.\\" But I was heartbroken along with Katniss, just as I, as a reader, loved Peeta right along with her. I see so much criticism of her behavior, how she's too self-centered and whiny. What I see is someone handling things that most of us cannot possibly imagine truly having to experience. She does the best she can, and at the points where she breaks down and can't function anymore, I challenge anyone to really think they would do better. I don't understand the self-centered comments. I've seen them in reviews of all three books, and I don't know where it comes from. I see Katniss putting other people before herself all the time. When she volunteered to take her sister's place in the Hunger Games, for instance, in book one. When she vowed to save Peeta at the cost of her own life in book two. Her defense of enemy civilians in book three. She is a strong, principled person. Very believable and, I think, admirable.<br /><br />One moment in the book resonated deeply with me. When she commented that Peeta would never be the same again, and Johanna pointed out that all of them were changed by their experiences. I found it extremely poignant and just a very deep truth about life.<br /><br />I was horrified by all of the death I read, but I was supposed to be. To do otherwise with this book would have cheapened it.<br /><br />In the end, she still had Haymitch as a father figure, and Peeta was there for her, if a bit more broken than before. Gale had to go away, because was there really any possible way she could ever get past associating him with her sister's death?  I also feel that Peeta, the boy with the bread, throughout these books, represents hope.  In the end, that was what she needed the most. She was able to have children knowing they would not be forced to participate in The Hunger Games. It was a good ending.

0375869026|5.0|When I first received \\"Wonder\\" I was afraid that the story would be far too cliched and stereotyped to be an engaging, enjoyable, or even tolerable book.  While the plot line is a tad bit obvious, R. J. Palacio was able to keep his characters fresh and quite likeable. The author deftly weaves together the story from eight various points of view,  This gives the reader an amazing connection to the book.  To know how each person perceived a singular event gave Auggie's story an incredible depth.<br /><br />The main character is August (Auggie) Pullman who was born with a severe facial deformity.  Though self conscious of it all of his life, it isn't until he attends a private school that he really begins to see himself through the eyes of others.  His mother had coddled him until now, and suddenly he is pushed into a world where cruelty often takes place more than compassion. It's 5th grade, and Auggie's parents want him to attend a real school, to mix with other children, to experience life outside the shelter of his home and home schooling.  Auggie agrees, and this begins his painful yet humorous and uplifting story.  It was difficult to be passive when reading about the cruel bullying and terrible jokes Auggie is subjected to.  For anyone who was ever on the receiving end of such behavior, it's easy to identify the internal pain he has to deal with. Yet somehow, Auggie manages to proceed with a maturity and grace that speak of an understanding of human nature far beyond his years.<br /><br />In the end, the real Auggie is finally seen by his classmates.  Though the lesson the author wants us to learn (It's what's on the inside that counts the most.) is obvious from the first page, following Auggie on his journey was a wonderful, funny and tearful ride.  \\"Wonder\\" packs quite an emotional punch. B000058983|5.0|Love was a late 60's L.A. group popular around town for their r&b garage-folk sound and multi-racial line up. Veterans of the California scene, they were the band that Jim Morrison said he wanted the Doors to be like. Signed to Elektra Records in 1966 they released two albums and several singles including Burt Bacharach's \\"My Little Red Book\\" and the transcendent proto-punk masterpiece \\"7 and 7 Is\\".<br />Released in late 1967, it was Love's third album Forever Changes that cemented their legacy in the timeless annals of rock history. Although doubtlessly influenced by Sgt. Pepper's, Forever Changes was more of Love's Pet Sounds in the sense that it's deeply personal message and airy musical urgency is similar to the Beach Boys' crowning achievement. Not only that, but many of the session musicians on Pet Sounds were present on Forever Changes.<br />Pushing garage rock to the side, Love's lead man Arthur Lee opted for a studio orchestra to create the backdrop to his melodic and haunting songs. Some of the arrangements were even rumored to have been written by Neil Young.<br />Pastoral string arrangements, mariachi horns and harpsichord accompany the acoustic framework of the songs with occasional jolts of electric guitar. The lyrics reflect a menacing vibe looming in the times as Arthur Lee's gentle and pleading voice almost sounds like Johnny Mathis.<br />Vincent Rodriguez, former music critic for the Dallas Morning News said of Forever Changes, \\"This dark, innovative and melancholy masterpiece squashed the 1960s utopianism - if it ever existed - three years before the decade ended.\\"<br />Not making much of a chart impact here in the states, it was much more well received in England. Forever Changes has stood the test of time and become a land mark album for the ages. NME rated Forever Changes #6 on their list of greatest albums of all time, and Rolling Stone ranked it at #40.

Between 1963 and 1966 they had released several singles and recorded two albums for Australian labels. It was their successful single \\"Spicks and Specks\\" that fueled them to take off for England and become instant stars.<br />After auditioning for and being signed to Polydor Records, the Bee Gees were hailed as \\"The Most Significant New Talent Of 1967\\". They released 3 singles to herald in their new/first international big time album. Classics \\"New York Mining Disaster 1941\\", \\"To Love Somebody\\" and \\"Holiday\\" tore up the singles charts, landing Bee Gees 1st at No. 7 on the U.S. charts and No. 8 in the UK.<br />The album featured 14 tracks, all originals, chock full of progressive pop, melancholy harmonies, sweet orchestral arrangements and shades of psychedelic rock. Rhythm and blues influences abound as well; \\"To Love Somebody\\" was originally written for and intended as an Otis Redding song. B0000009RT|5.0|When legendary record producer Tom Wilson (Bob Dylan, Simon & Garfunkel, Velvet Underground) signed Frank Zappa's band The Mothers in 1966, he thought they were just another white blues band he could easily market and make a quick buck off of.<br />After hearing their first two recorded songs \\"Any Way The Wind Blows\\" and \\"Who Are The Brain Police?\\" he realized there was a lot more to this band and quickly got on the phone to make sure they had the money to complete \\"Freak Out!\\" the way they wanted. They ended up with the first double album for a debut release.<br />The title to the album was inspired by the increasing number of long-haired freaky people that had been flocking to L.A.'s Sunset Strip since the early 60's and also to the Mothers of Inventions shows. The Mothers live and television appearances were all out happenings of bizarrely dressed weirdo dancers and gave them the reputation of being THE Where-It's-At L.A. band alongside The Turtles, The Byrds, The Doors and Love.<br />Although Freak Out! didn't do well on the charts in the U.S., Paul McCartney has been quoted as saying that the album was a big influence on The Beatles' recording of Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band. The album was obviously an underground classic and is still considered one of the most ambitious releases of its day.<br />With Frank Zappa's signature cynical and satirical lyrics not quite as strong as on future releases, Freak Out! spans the spectrum of pop and rock musical influences of the mid-60's with the garage rock of \\"Hungry Freaks, Daddy\\"; the orchestral ballad arrangements of \\"How Could I Be Such A Fool?\\"; the zany bubblegum pop of \\"Wowie Zowie\\"; the civil criticism stomp of \\"Trouble Of Everyday\\" and the all out avant-garde freak-out of \\"Help, I'm A Rock\\" followed by \\"The Return Of The Son Of Monster Magnet\\".<br />For an hour long dose of skillfully composed mid-60's freakiness crusted over with a complete mockery for social conformity and dissatisfaction with just about anything (is this part one of punk rock?), find Freak Out! immediately.ut anything (is this part one of punk rock?), find Freak Out! immediately.

0670023329|5.0|Blaine Harden has written an adsorbing account of Shin's escape from Labor Camp 14 in North Korea. It is remarkable that Shin was able to escape, as fate played a significant role. In this book Harden refers to victims of labor (concentration) camps, such as the Holocaust Jews, as mained human beings. All those interned have a common bond, they were dehumanized by the experience and years afterward are still feeling the affects of their incarceration. Shin, having been born in the camp, never received any positive guiding life principals, only negative ones. It made me think about an age old question, \\"Is man inherently good or evil?\\" How could the guards and authorities treat Shin so badly? Physical and mental torture, administered with intimidation apparently rule in the camps. In the North Korean philosophy the sins of the father's are visited upon the children for three generations. In this account Harden does an excellent job of relating Shin's brutalization in the camp, his flight to China, his journey to South Korea, and finally the United States. Harden interviewed Shin many times and Shin came to trust him, something that was hard for him to do. It is a remarkable account and relates well the  indifference and totalitarian nature of North Korea. It is a depressing as well as a joyous book to read because it highlights the resiliency of the human spirit. B000002LQR|5.0|...I would take my portable player and this CD everywhere.  It was one of my most frequently played, racked up miles and nicks galore, and in the 90's, before I finally ripped it to MP3, I would go through THREE physical copies of it, resurfaced and re-scratched again.  Even then, I ripped to my hard drive scratches that were there on \\"Sir Psycho Sexy\\" and had to download it.<br /><br />All this because as albums go, it was THAT essential and personal to me.  The fusion of rock, funk, punk, and rap opened up my perception to other genres of music, and \\"Under the Bridge\\" helped me understand the plight of an addict in my family at the time.<br /><br />Any way you slice it, Blood Sugar Sex Magik ranks right up there with the other major rock albums released between 1989-1994, albums that would be either mangum opuses and/or commercial breakthroughs for these artists and explode the commercial grunge and alt rock scene into what it became.  Think \\"Disintegration\\" by The Cure, \\"Ten\\" by Pearl Jam, \\"Simaese Dream\\" by Smashing Pumpkins, even \\"Nevermind\\" by Nirvana: all dominating airwaves and MTV rotation after seminal albums by Sonic Youth and The Pixies paved the way.<br /><br />In later years, it would all be watered down, become much more commercial, and fade away.  The Chili Peppers themselves still consistently put out quality albums...I wouldn't argue weakness on \\"Stadium Arcaduim\\" or even \\"By the Way.\\"  They're all definitely worthy listens, but whether tapping introspection, funky grooves, or hard rock, the Peppers would never again make something that had the all the coherence, inspiration, reflection, and song-to-song excellence of Blood Sugar Sex Magik.  This was their creative, artistic, and commercial zenith, and it came at an exciting time when so many other great bands were peaking and hitting their stride.

I know I didn't get into this for literary rewards, I just want to fantasize about Christian Grey because guys like him are as real as a tap-dancing unicorn (seriously, an Adonis billionaire at the age of 27? Please, even Mark Zuckerberg has a pot belly).<br /><br />However, I would strongly advice you to stop reading after the first book (especially if you enjoyed it). I get that this is based on Twilight's fan fiction, but man does it get annoying very fast. What started as a sorta believable narration from a 21-year old morphed into the voice of the author's inner old cougar. This is clearly her middle-aged fantasy and she is not modest about deploying every cliche.<br /><br />It seems like E.L. James has literally ran out of ways to shake up the vocabulary and sentence structures from the first book. In fact, it started mirroring Twilight's simpering speech of \\"he's too good for me, he is so handsome, I can't believe he's mine, oh I love him so much\\" prose to ad nauseam. If I closed my eyes I would have sworn two 13-year old girls got together and compiled a super list of the perfect guy. Christian is handsome, rich and glowers a lot--that is as complex as his character gets. Ana, though charmingly stubborn and somewhat smart in the first novel, has become an idiot lacking in the simplest of common sense. I am a woman in my 20s, college educated, and I find Ana's growing stupidity very out of touch and aggravating. This is what the author thinks 20-something year olds do versus understanding how we really operate. Ana gets herself in trouble in not-so-cute or sympathetic ways while Christian growls and suddenly shows up everywhere at the drop of a hat (doesn't a man like that work?). Christian also needs a new pair of pants because he's wearing the socks off of those flannels for the past three books.<br /><br />The s*x? Let's not even go there. Ladies, we can all agree that unless you are in the adult film industry, a big \\"o\\" every s-i-n-g-l-e time is just acting. Not even good acting because we at least pace it out (don't lie, we've all been there). Perhaps Mr. James should take notice. It repeated like a bad porn formula from the 90s after the first book...curious, does the author watch lots of those? It sure sounds like it, you old minx.

This novel is tedious, meandering, needlessly vulgar, and does not advance the story in any significant way at all. I find the land of Westeros fascinating, but I don't care enough to follow the life story of every minor noble and haberdasher in it, which the author seems intent upon doing. 0817439390|4.0|This book is the must for anybody who starts with photography. The main thing about any type of photography digital or film is EXPOSURE. Bryan Peterson is very experienced photographer and from his years of shooting he delivers a lot of practical knowledge. I like his focus on the photo-triangle that is combination of 3 factors in an exposure: Shutter Speed - Aperture - ISO (censor sensitivity to light). However, he is oldie type of photographer and he kind of stuck with old practices and does not really trust new technologies in digital photography. He mainly suggests to switch almost everything to manual operation in your advanced DSLR. In addition, I did not like his opinion that digital histograms were heavily over-rated, he also did not say much about the importance of white balance (WB). The digital histogram is the most critical tool for judging as how your data is spread in your dynamic range. I use it all the time whenever I can while shooting. And of course, when you do image editing in Camera Raw, it tells you what was clipped and it guides you to your best image. I like his emphasis on the importance of where to take your exposure meter readings. This actually could make or break your exposure especially in the high contrast scenario. However, his advise for taking meter reading from the \\"Sky Brothers\\" or \\"Mr. Green Jeans\\" does not work all the time and could cause underexposures from the former one and overexposure from the later one. I have tried his advises and sometimes it worked. It's just good to know, so you have to use your own judgment. I wish we can always find 18% gray subject in our images to meter them for an exposure, that's why a gray card is a useful tool, however, to implement one in all your subject shootings is next to impossible as you need to hurry as I have to while shooting my grandkids. Otherwise this book is very valuable tool and must be used as a reference if you want to really understand the main subject of photography.

I decided to give it a try, fully expecting to turn it off within 20 minutes or so. It's no wonder that Francis Ford Coppola had his hand in both of these pics -- he was of course the director of \\"Apocalypse Now\\" and the executive producer of \\"The Secret Garden.\\"<br /><br />Anyway, the cinematography is breathtaking and the music is wonderful.<br /><br />PLOT SPOILER!!<br /><br />To be brief, the story's about a young spoilied English girl, orphaned in India, who comes home to live on her uncle's vast estate in the early 1900's. The girl, Mary, finds herself trapped in a mysterious, colossal manor -- almost a castle -- tyranically managed by a life-stifling witch, Mrs. Medlock, in the frequent absence of her uncle, Lord Cravin. Because Mary is highly intelligent, independant and sly she is easily able to reconnoiter the manor and learn its forbidding secrets. The biggest secret is that her aunt died in childbirth about ten years before, but her son, Colin, still lives there, albeit confined to a bed, sickly and unable to walk. Her uncle evidently never healed from this heartbreak and this explains his frequent absences.<br /><br />Mary finds a secret garden in her explorations, a hidden garden closed up and neglected since her aunt's death. After meeting the sickly and sad Colin, Mary inevitably finds a way to sneak him into the beautiful garden along with her pal Dickon. She instinctively senses that Colin isn't as sick as everyone is convinced he is; she knows the best thing for Colin would be to get him out of the dreary castle and the oppressive clutches of Mrs. Medlock. Mary and her secret garden are the keys to restoring health, life and freedom to Colin, Lord Cravin and the gloomy manor.<br /><br />END SPOILER<br /><br />\\"The Secret Garden\\" is kind of a 1990's version of the outstanding \\"Pollyanna\\" with Hayley Mills (1960). Now when I think of James Sallis' Drive, I don't think this at all. 0345803485|4.0|After hearing all of the hype about this book, I decided to read it.  As a feminist, I was curious/worried about the themes of this book and whether or not is was the typical sexualizing and romanticizing of male dominance/violence that women (and men) have been fed in media for a long time.  Overall, I would say that I enjoyed the book, but I wouldn't champion this book as feminist lit either.  First, the book is not deep prose, but it's an interesting read that kept me wanting to know more about what each of the main characters was going to do and how each character was going to respond to what the other did.  Second, the sex scenes are hot.  Yes, this book has a lot of BDSM, but it's about more than that.  I have never been one to pathologize the BDSM lifestyle (not knowing a lot about it), but I think EL James treats this lifestyle fairly and allows the lead female protagonist (Ana Steele) realistic reactions of curiosity, fear, embarrassment, outrage, and arousal to the sexual acts that the male protagonist (Christian Grey) suggests.  Importantly, James also makes Christian Grey more than a sexual deviant (with a rather complicated history) and does not write Ana Steele as simply a naive virgin who does whatever her male sexual partner demands of her.  The attraction between the two is a little too quick for my liking, but I got into the relationship as the book progressed (and particularly liked the flirty e-mail exchanges between the two).  Third, with regard to the gender issues, I was ambivalent.  I applaud James for writing a female character who really enjoys sex and who (mostly) asks for what she wants (and does not want) from her partner.  As noted above, I also liked that Ana's reactions to this man seemed mostly realistic (without being universally positive or negative).  I still haven't made up my mind about whether or not BDSM is inherently sexist (is it a product of a culture that enforces male sexual dominance and female submission or is it simplya sexual preference that has dominant and submissive roles?); I'll leave that for others to decide as they learn about this sexual preference.  That aside, Ana was still a little too accepting of Christian's possessive jealousy for my liking.  I could have done without the \\"possessive jealousy as love\\" message that the book promoted.<br /><br />One last note about something that didn't really affect my enjoyment of the book one way or another but just something that nagged at me was how EL James was a little unrealistic with the sex.  Come on, can Christian really have sex that frequently?  Hasn't EL James heard of a refractory period?  And is Ana really going to orgasm THAT often in that many positions with that little clitoral stimulation?  I'm not saying it's impossible, but it just seemed pretty far-fetched.  When I'm reading about sex, I like the details to remain physiologically sound.  :-)

The reader meets Marco and Celia as children - children who do not know each other but who are bound to a bet that they will not find out about until they are older and that they cannot break.  This bet is waged between two powerful magicians who pit different methods for teaching (actual) magic against each other in the form of a game between their pupils.  The Night Circus is the venue for an elaborate and beautiful magic competition between Marco and Celia, who fall deeply in love as the details of the bet unfold.  Marco and Celia are completely likeable characters who, when together, share my favorite scenes in the book. I wasn't entirely satisfied with the ending, but it's a difficult story to end given the parameters the Morgenstern sets.

1433523922|5.0|After much research and comparing, I decided to take Crossway's ESV Study Bible over the leading competitors, such as the Oxford Annotated Bible. The ESV translation is one of the newer on the market, and is already praised for its excellent balance between poetry and literality. It also does not seem to have as many problems with gender-neutrality as the NRSV. One feature I find lacking, however, is that the places where the Tetragrammaton appears are rendered as The LORD - the same rendering is used in places where Adonai and other names appear; and that makes it unclear when YHWH is used (nota bene: this information does not appear in the annotations either). A fairly serious flaw to me, but most modern bibles seem to be suffering from this problem, so I guess I'll just have to accept it. Another flaw: this version lacks the Deuterocanon and Apocrypha.<br />Note: the Lutheran Study Bible also uses the ESV translation.<br /><br />Now, the Study Bible itself. Great and helpful annotations (modern, evangelical, reformed perspective, of course; but this is expected), a lot of notes on the historical context. Illustrations are beautiful and really help visualise the situations present in the text. The information at the beginning of every book is also great, containing timelines, historical info, theological notes, notes about the literary qualities, rhetorical devices used, etc. One great feature every printed copy has is a code for Crossway's online version of the Study Bible, with annotations, reading plans and other features on hand.<br /><br />As for the build quality, I opted for the leather-bound, larger print version. I can't say if the tru-tone fake leather is just as good or better (I haven't tried it), but I am very satisfied with these materials. The larger print also seems as if it was worth it. Though the Bible text itself immediately looks large, the annotations use a smaller font, and this is where a larger-print version helps.<br /><br />P.S. My copydid not suffer from misprinted pages, as many reviewers seem to be talking about.
